Views on the Necessity of Prophethood
The author presents three views on the necessity of prophethood and the principle of divine wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main views (Mu'tazila, philosophers, and some theologians) on the necessity of prophethood and the principle that God cannot act foolishly.

Core Idea
Prophethood is necessary because its absence would imply foolishness in God.

This lesson introduces the central debate: is it obligatory upon God to send prophets? The author presents the Mu'tazili and philosophers' positions, then notes that some theologians argued it is a requirement of God's wisdom. This lays the groundwork for the detailed proofs that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation to grasp the main claims.
Notice the three distinct groups mentioned.
2
Identify the three views
List the Mu'tazili view, the philosophers' view, and the theologians' extension. Note how each justifies the necessity.
The theologians base their argument on God's wisdom and the impossibility of foolishness.
3
Understand the key principle
Focus on the statement that it is impossible for God to act without purpose. Connect this to the broader kalam concept of divine attributes.
This principle is used to argue that prophethood must exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأنبياء، فيجب على الله تعالى عند المعتزلة لكونه لطفاً وصلاحاً للعباد، وعند الفلاسفة لكونه سبباً للخير العام المستحيل تركه في الحكمة (١) والعناية الإلهية، وإلى هذا ذهب جمع من المتكلمين بما وراء النهي وقالوا : إنها من مقتضيات حكمة الباري عز وجل فيستحيل ان لا يوجد لاستحالة السفه عليه. كما أن ما علم الله وقوعه يجب أن يقع لاستحالة الجهل عليه ، ثم طولوا في ذلك وعولوا على ضروب من الاستدلال مرجعها إلى ما ذكرنا من لزوم السفه والعبث كما في خلق الأغذية والأدوية التي لا تتميز عن السموم المهلكة إلا بتجارب لا يتجاسر عليها العقلاء، ولا يفي بها الأعمار، وخلق الأبدان التي ليس لها بدون الغذاء إلا الفناء، وخلق نوع الإنسان المفتقر في البقاء إلى اجتماع لا ينتظم بدون بعثة الأنبياء، وكخلق العقل المائل إلى المحاسن ، النافر عن القبائح، الجازم بأن شرفه وكماله في العلم بتفاصيل ذلك، والعمل بمقتضياتها من الامتثال والاجتناب، وأنه لا يستقل بجميع ذلك على التفصيل، بل يفتقر إلى بيان ممن أوجدها ودعا إلى الإتيان بالبعض منها، والانتهاء عن البعض كالمجمل من الخطاب، فإن خلق العقل مائلاً إلى المحاسن، نافراً عن القبائح بمنزلة الخطاب في كونه دليلاً على الأمر والنهي اللذين هما من الصفات القائمة بذاته تعالى، إذ لا معنى لهما سوى الدعوة إلى المباشرة والامتناع وكما في جعل بعض الأفعال بحيث قد يحمد عاقبته ، فيجب . وقد يذم ، فيحرم ، كالصوم مثلاً، فلو لم يكن له بيان من الشارع لكان في ذلك إباحة ترك الواجب، وإباحة مباشرة المحظور وهو خارج عن الحكمة، فظهر بهذه الوجوه، وأمثالها أنه لابد من النبي البتة (۳) ، ولهذا كان في كل عصر للعقلاء نبي أو من يخلفه في إقامة الدليل السمعي، وكان الغالب على المتمسكين بالشرائع سلوك طريق الحق وسبيل النجاة والرشاد مع اشتغالهم باكتساب أسباب المعاش، وخلو أكثرهم عن صناعة النظر وحذاقة الذهن، وعلى الفلاسفة المتشبثين بأذيال العقل العدول عن الصواب والوقوع في الضلال مع رجاحة عقولهم، ودقة أنظارهم وإقبالهم بالكلية على البحث عن المعارف الإلهية والعلوم اليقينية، وأنت خبير بأن في ترويج أمثال هذا المقال توسيع مجال الاعتزال فإنهم لا يعنون بالوجوب على الله تعالى سوى أن تركه لقبحه مخل بالحكمة ومظنة لاستحقاق المذمة، فالحق أن البعثة لطف من الله تعالى ورحمة يحسن فعلها ولا يقبح تركها على ما هو المذهب في سائر الألطاف، ولا تبتنى على استحقاق من المبعوث واجتماع أسباب وشروط فيه، بل الله تعالى يختص برحمته من يشاء من عباده وهو أعلم حيث يجعل رسالته.
Prophet: It is obligatory upon God according to the Mu'tazila because it is a grace and good for the servants, and according to the philosophers because it is a cause for the general good which is impossible to abandon in wisdom and divine care. To this went a group of theologians beyond prohibition and said: They are from the requirements of the wisdom of the Creator, so it is impossible that they not exist due to the impossibility of foolishness upon Him. Just as what God knows will occur must occur due to the impossibility of ignorance upon Him. Then they elaborated and relied on various types of proofs whose reference is to what we mentioned of the necessity of foolishness and futility, as in the creation of foods and medicines that are not distinguished from deadly poisons except by experiments that rational people dare not undertake and lifetimes are insufficient for, and the creation of bodies that without food have only annihilation, and the creation of the human species that depends for survival on a society that cannot be organized without sending prophets, and as the creation of the intellect inclined towards good things, averse to evil things, which judges that its honor and perfection lie in knowledge of the details of that, and acting upon their requirements of compliance and avoidance, and that it is not independent in all that in detail, but needs an explanation from the One who brought them into existence and called for some of them and forbade others, like an ambiguous address. For the creation of the intellect inclined towards good and averse to evil is like the address in being a proof of the command and prohibition which are from the attributes established in His essence, since their meaning is nothing but the call to do or abstain. And as in making some actions so that their consequence may be praised, so they become obligatory, or blamed, so they become prohibited, like fasting for example. If there were no explanation from the Lawgiver, that would be permitting the abandonment of the obligatory and permitting direct commission of the forbidden, which is outside of wisdom. Thus it appears by these aspects and their like that there must absolutely be a prophet. For this reason, in every age rational people have a prophet or one who succeeds him in establishing the revealed proof. And most of those who adhere to the laws follow the way of truth and the path of salvation and right guidance while occupied with earning means of livelihood, and most of them lack the craft of speculation and sharpness of mind, whereas the philosophers who cling to the tails of reason deviate from correctness and fall into error despite the soundness of their intellects, the precision of their speculations, and their total devotion to research on divine knowledge and certain sciences. And you are experienced that promoting such articles expands the field of Mu'tazilism, for they do not mean by obligation upon God except that its abandonment due to its ugliness violates wisdom and is a place for deserving blame. So the truth is that the sending is a grace from God and mercy; it is good to do and not ugly to omit, as is the doctrine in all other graces. It is not based on the merit of the sent one or the gathering of causes and conditions in him, but God singles out with His mercy whom He wills from His servants, and He knows best where to place His message.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage presents various arguments for the necessity of sending prophets. It begins by stating the Mu'tazili view that it is obligatory upon God because it is a grace and benefit for the servants. Then it mentions the philosophers' view that it is necessary for the general good, impossible to omit due to divine wisdom and care. Then it notes that some theologians went further, claiming it is a requirement of God's wisdom and is as necessary as His foreknowledge necessitates occurrences. They provided proofs based on the avoidance of futility and foolishness. The author then elaborates on their proofs: in creation of foods and medicines that require experience, bodies needing food, human society needing prophets, and the intellect's need for clarification from the Creator. They draw an analogy between the intellect's inclination and a divine address. If there were no revealed explanation, it would permit violation of duties, which contradicts wisdom. Thus, a prophet is absolutely necessary. Historically, every age has had a prophet or successor. The author notes that those who adhere to religious laws are often on the right path despite lacking sophisticated reasoning, whereas philosophers who rely solely on reason often go astray. Finally, the author refutes the Mu'tazili notion of obligation, stating that the correct view is that the sending of prophets is a grace and mercy from God, not an obligation. It is good but not ugly to omit, and it is not based on any merit in the recipient but purely on God's choice.

Key Terms
وجوب
لطف
حكمة
سفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari view is that prophethood is obligatory upon God.
Correction: Only the Mu'tazila hold it is obligatory; the Ash'aris later clarify it is a grace, not an obligation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the theologians' argument that prophethood must exist?
A) God's general grace for all believers
B) The impossibility of foolishness upon God
C) The need for human society to be organized
D) The example of philosophers

Key Takeaway
The necessity of prophethood is argued from divine wisdom: its absence would imply foolishness, which is impossible for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that prophethood is a necessary manifestation of God's wisdom, would you give the same attention to the Prophet's message as you do to your daily decisions? What would change if you recognised that ignoring prophethood is an insult to God's wisdom?
?
The theologians argued that without prophethood, God's wisdom would be meaningless. Do you live as if God's wisdom is real, or do you sometimes act as if your own wisdom is sufficient? What would it take for you to submit entirely to the guidance sent through the prophets?
?
How often do you reflect on the fact that God's sending of prophets is not a 'nice extra' but a requirement of His very nature? Does this conviction move you to obedience, or do you treat prophethood as a historical footnote?

The Rational Proofs for Prophethood
The author provides four concrete rational proofs that necessitate prophethood, drawn from creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four specific proofs from creation that demonstrate the need for a prophet, as presented by the theologians.

Core Idea
Creation contains signs that cannot be understood without prophetic guidance.

The author now elaborates on the proofs that the theologians used to show that prophethood is required by divine wisdom. These proofs are drawn from the natural world, human physiology, society, and the intellect itself. Each points to the necessity of a divine messenger to complete what reason alone cannot reach.

Guided Path
1
Locate the four proofs
Read the Arabic and identify the four examples: foods/medicines, bodies, society, and intellect. Note how each demonstrates a need for external guidance.
The first example is about distinguishing beneficial from harmful things.
2
Analyze the intellect proof
The fourth proof is the most detailed: the intellect is inclined toward good and averse to evil, but cannot discern all details. This is like a general address needing explanation.
This leads to the idea that divine revelation provides the specifics.
3
Connect to wisdom
Understand that without a prophet, the creation would be futile (عبث), which contradicts God's wisdom.
The author says that permitting violation of duties would be 'outside wisdom'.

Prophet: It is obligatory upon God according to the Mu'tazila because it is a grace and good for the servants, and according to the philosophers because it is a cause for the general good which is impossible to abandon in wisdom and divine care. To this went a group of theologians beyond prohibition and said: They are from the requirements of the wisdom of the Creator, so it is impossible that they not exist due to the impossibility of foolishness upon Him. Just as what God knows will occur must occur due to the impossibility of ignorance upon Him. Then they elaborated and relied on various types of proofs whose reference is to what we mentioned of the necessity of foolishness and futility, as in the creation of foods and medicines that are not distinguished from deadly poisons except by experiments that rational people dare not undertake and lifetimes are insufficient for, and the creation of bodies that without food have only annihilation, and the creation of the human species that depends for survival on a society that cannot be organized without sending prophets, and as the creation of the intellect inclined towards good things, averse to evil things, which judges that its honor and perfection lie in knowledge of the details of that, and acting upon their requirements of compliance and avoidance, and that it is not independent in all that in detail, but needs an explanation from the One who brought them into existence and called for some of them and forbade others, like an ambiguous address. For the creation of the intellect inclined towards good and averse to evil is like the address in being a proof of the command and prohibition which are from the attributes established in His essence, since their meaning is nothing but the call to do or abstain. And as in making some actions so that their consequence may be praised, so they become obligatory, or blamed, so they become prohibited, like fasting for example. If there were no explanation from the Lawgiver, that would be permitting the abandonment of the obligatory and permitting direct commission of the forbidden, which is outside of wisdom. Thus it appears by these aspects and their like that there must absolutely be a prophet. For this reason, in every age rational people have a prophet or one who succeeds him in establishing the revealed proof. And most of those who adhere to the laws follow the way of truth and the path of salvation and right guidance while occupied with earning means of livelihood, and most of them lack the craft of speculation and sharpness of mind, whereas the philosophers who cling to the tails of reason deviate from correctness and fall into error despite the soundness of their intellects, the precision of their speculations, and their total devotion to research on divine knowledge and certain sciences. And you are experienced that promoting such articles expands the field of Mu'tazilism, for they do not mean by obligation upon God except that its abandonment due to its ugliness violates wisdom and is a place for deserving blame. So the truth is that the sending is a grace from God and mercy; it is good to do and not ugly to omit, as is the doctrine in all other graces. It is not based on the merit of the sent one or the gathering of causes and conditions in him, but God singles out with His mercy whom He wills from His servants, and He knows best where to place His message.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage presents various arguments for the necessity of sending prophets. It begins by stating the Mu'tazili view that it is obligatory upon God because it is a grace and benefit for the servants. Then it mentions the philosophers' view that it is necessary for the general good, impossible to omit due to divine wisdom and care. Then it notes that some theologians went further, claiming it is a requirement of God's wisdom and is as necessary as His foreknowledge necessitates occurrences. They provided proofs based on the avoidance of futility and foolishness. The author then elaborates on their proofs: in creation of foods and medicines that require experience, bodies needing food, human society needing prophets, and the intellect's need for clarification from the Creator. They draw an analogy between the intellect's inclination and a divine address. If there were no revealed explanation, it would permit violation of duties, which contradicts wisdom. Thus, a prophet is absolutely necessary. Historically, every age has had a prophet or successor. The author notes that those who adhere to religious laws are often on the right path despite lacking sophisticated reasoning, whereas philosophers who rely solely on reason often go astray. Finally, the author refutes the Mu'tazili notion of obligation, stating that the correct view is that the sending of prophets is a grace and mercy from God, not an obligation. It is good but not ugly to omit, and it is not based on any merit in the recipient but purely on God's choice.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to trust your intellect in all matters of right and wrong — but when have you faced a situation where your mind could not decide the best course without external guidance? What does that reveal about your need for prophetic teachings?
?

Which of these, if any, have you ever truly pondered as a sign pointing to the need for a prophet? If not, why do you think you haven't?
?
If you were left solely with your reason, without any divine guidance, would you be confident that you could avoid all harmful actions and fulfill all necessary obligations? Where has your intellect let you down before, and how has prophetic guidance saved you? Commit today to identifying one area where you rely more on your own reasoning than on what the prophet taught, and seek the correct guidance.

Prophethood
intermediate
7 min
Historical Evidence and the Correct View
The author concludes with historical evidence and the Ash'ari position that prophethood is a grace, not an obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical argument that prophets have guided people correctly, and the Ash'ari position that prophethood is a grace from God, not an obligation.

Core Idea
History shows prophets succeed where reason alone fails; prophethood is a grace, not a duty on God.

After presenting the rational proofs, the author now turns to historical observation: those who follow prophets are guided rightly, while philosophers who rely solely on reason fall into error. This leads to the final clarification that the correct Islamic view is that sending prophets is a grace (lutf) and mercy from God, not an obligation. The passage also refutes the Mu'tazili notion of obligation.

Guided Path
1
Observe the historical contrast
Read the comparison between followers of religious laws and the philosophers. Note the outcomes: the former succeed, the latter err despite their intellect.
This is an empirical argument from the author's perspective.
2
Understand the expansion of Mu'tazilism
The author warns that promoting the idea of obligation on God expands Mu'tazilism. Recognize the theological sensitivity.
The Mu'tazila mean by obligation that omission would be ugly (qabīḥ).
3
Grasp the final position
The correct view: ba'tha is a grace and mercy; it is good (ḥasan) to do, but not ugly to omit. It is not based on the merit of the prophet but on God's choice.
This aligns with Ash'ari theology.

وقالوا : إنها من مقتضيات حكمة الباري عز وجل فيستحيل ان لا يوجد لاستحالة السفه عليه. كما أن ما علم الله وقوعه يجب أن يقع لاستحالة الجهل عليه ، ثم طولوا في ذلك وعولوا على ضروب من الاستدلال مرجعها إلى ما بمنزلة الخطاب في كونه دليلاً على الأمر والنهي اللذين هما من الصفات القائمة بذاته تعالى، إذ لا معنى لهما سوى الدعوة إلى المباشرة والامتناع وكما في جعل بعض الأفعال بحيث قد يحمد عاقبته ، فيجب . وقد يذم ، فيحرم ، كالصوم مثلاً، فلو لم يكن له بيان من الشارع لكان في ذلك إباحة ترك الواجب، وإباحة مباشرة المحظور وهو خارج عن الحكمة، فظهر بهذه الوجوه، وأمثالها أنه لابد من النبي البتة (۳) ، ولهذا كان في كل عصر للعقلاء نبي أو من يخلفه في إقامة الدليل السمعي، وكان الغالب على المتمسكين بالشرائع سلوك طريق الحق وسبيل النجاة والرشاد مع اشتغالهم باكتساب أسباب المعاش، وخلو أكثرهم عن صناعة النظر وحذاقة الذهن، وعلى الفلاسفة المتشبثين بأذيال العقل العدول عن الصواب والوقوع في الضلال مع رجاحة عقولهم، ودقة أنظارهم وإقبالهم بالكلية على البحث عن المعارف الإلهية والعلوم اليقينية، وأنت خبير بأن في ترويج أمثال هذا المقال To this went a group of theologians beyond prohibition and said: They are from the requirements of the wisdom of the Creator, so it is impossible that they not exist due to the impossibility of foolishness upon Him. Prophethood
beginner

Objection 1: Knowledge of the Sender
First objection and refutation: knowledge of the sender.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection that prophethood requires prior knowledge of God and its refutation through proofs or necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
Prophethood does not require prior knowledge; God can create the means to know Him.

The first objection claims that prophethood presupposes knowledge of God, which seems impossible without revelation. But the refutation shows that God can establish rational proofs or create direct necessary knowledge. This lesson explores that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage for objection 1 and its translation. Notice the claim that prophethood depends on prior knowledge of God.
2
Identify the refutation
The refutation offers two possibilities: establishing proofs or creating necessary knowledge. Consider how this bypasses the objection.
3
Reflect on the lesson
Think about why this objection fails and how it reinforces the possibility of prophethood.
This shows that God can make His messengers certain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وللمنكرين . شبه إحداها : أنها تتوقف على علم المبعوث بأن الباعث هو الله تعالى، ولا سبيل إليه ، ورد بجواز نصب الأدلة، أو خلق العلم الضروري.. الثاني: أنها عبث، لأن ما حسن عقلاً يفعل، وما قبح يترك، وما لم يحسن ولا يقبح يفعل حسب المصلحة، ورد بأنها تعاضد العقل فيما يستقل وتعاونه فيما لا يستقل الاحتمال فيما يظن، وتكون الطريق فيما لا يدرك مع أن التفويض إلى العقول المتفاوتة مظنة اختلال النظام. الثالث: أن مبناها على التكليف بما لا ينتفع به العبد لتضرره، ولا المعبود لتعاليه مع ما فيه من شغل السر عن التوجه التام، ورد بأن نفعه جداً غالب . الرابع : ان في الشرائع ما يشعر بأنها ليست من عند الله كأفعال الصلاة والحج، والوضوء والغسل، وغير ذلك من الأمور الخارجة عن قانون العقل (۲)، ورد بأنها
He said: And for the deniers are objections. One of them: That it depends on the sent one's knowledge that the sender is God, and there is no way to that. Refuted by the permissibility of establishing proofs, or creating necessary knowledge. The second: That it is futility, because what is rationally good is done, what is ugly is left, and what is neither good nor ugly is done according to interest. Refuted by that it assists reason in what it is independent and cooperates with it in what it is not independent [of] possibility in what is conjectured, and is the way in what is not perceived, while entrusting to differing intellects is a place for the disturbance of order. The third: That it is based on obligation by what the slave does not benefit from due to his harm, nor the worshipped due to His transcendence, along with what is in it of preoccupying the heart from complete attention. Refuted by that its benefit is greatly predominant. The fourth: That in the laws there is what indicates that they are not from God, such as the acts of prayer, pilgrimage, ablution, and washing, and other matters outside the law of reason (2). Refuted by that they are...
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage presents four objections from deniers of prophethood and their refutations. The first objection is that prophethood requires the prophet to know that the sender is God, which is impossible without prior knowledge. The refutation is that God can either establish proofs or create necessary knowledge to achieve this. The second objection is that prophethood is futile because reason alone can determine good and evil, so revelation is unnecessary. The refutation is that revelation assists reason in areas where reason is insufficient and removes doubt in conjectural matters, and it provides guidance in matters beyond sense perception. Furthermore, entrusting everything to differing intellects would lead to disorder. The third objection is that prophets impose obligations that bring no benefit to the slave or to God, and distract from devotion. The refutation is that the benefit of revelation is very great and predominant. The fourth objection, which is incomplete in the text, states that many religious practices seem irrational and indicate that they are not from God. The refutation, though cut off, likely explains that these are pure acts of worship known through revelation and beyond rational analysis.

B) Through establishing proofs or created necessary knowledge
C) Through inherited tradition
D) Through direct vision

Key Takeaway
God can make His prophets certain of their mission through proofs or direct knowledge, eliminating the need for prior revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If God can make His prophets certain through direct knowledge, what might that mean for your own search for truth? Will you stop relying solely on your own intellect and humbly ask Him to guide you with clarity?

Objection 2: Prophethood as Futility (ʿAbath)
Second objection and refutation: prophethood as futility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that prophethood is futile because reason suffices, and its refutation that revelation aids reason in areas it cannot reach.

Core Idea
Revelation complements reason; it does not replace it.

The second objection argues that since reason can determine good and evil, prophethood is unnecessary and thus futile. The refutation shows that reason has limits: revelation assists in speculative matters, removes doubts, and prevents societal disorder from differing intellects. Let's examine this.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic for objection 2. The objection claims prophethood is futile because reason alone can judge actions.
2
Understand the refutation's points
The refutation says revelation helps reason in what it is independent or not, handles possibilities, and is the way for imperceptible matters. Also, relying solely on reason among differering minds leads to disorder.
3
Reflect on reason's limitations
Consider how revelation provides guidance where reason is uncertain, especially in matters beyond sense perception.
This reinforces the necessity of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Objection 3: Obligation without Benefit
Third objection and refutation: no benefit in obligations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection that prophethood imposes obligations with no benefit to slave or God, and its refutation that the benefit is greatly predominant.

Core Idea
Divine obligations bring immense benefit even if not directly apparent.

The third objection claims that prophetic obligations bring no benefit to the slave (who may be harmed) nor to God (who is transcendent), and they distract from complete devotion. The refutation asserts that the benefit is vastly predominant. Let's see why.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic for objection 3. It argues that obligations are pointless and distracting.
2
Identify the refutation
The refutation simply states that the benefit is 'greatly predominant' (jiddan ghalib). Reflect on what that means.
3
Consider hidden benefits
Think about the unseen benefits of obedience and the greater good that outweighs apparent harm.
The benefit is not necessarily worldly but spiritual and eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وللمنكرين . شبه إحداها : أنها تتوقف على علم المبعوث بأن الباعث هو الله تعالى، ولا سبيل إليه ، ورد بجواز نصب الأدلة، أو خلق العلم الضروري.. الثاني: أنها عبث، لأن ما حسن عقلاً يفعل، وما قبح يترك، وما لم يحسن ولا يقبح يفعل حسب المصلحة، ورد بأنها تعاضد العقل فيما يستقل وتعاونه فيما لا يستقل الاحتمال فيما يظن، وتكون الطريق فيما لا يدرك مع أن التفويض إلى العقول المتفاوتة مظنة اختلال النظام. الثالث: أن مبناها على التكليف بما لا ينتفع به العبد لتضرره، ولا المعبود لتعاليه مع ما فيه من شغل السر عن التوجه التام، ورد بأن نفعه جداً غالب . الرابع : ان في الشرائع ما يشعر بأنها ليست من عند الله كأفعال الصلاة والحج، والوضوء والغسل، وغير ذلك من الأمور الخارجة عن قانون العقل (۲)، ورد بأنها
He said: And for the deniers are objections. One of them: That it depends on the sent one's knowledge that the sender is God, and there is no way to that. Refuted by the permissibility of establishing proofs, or creating necessary knowledge. The second: That it is futility, because what is rationally good is done, what is ugly is left, and what is neither good nor ugly is done according to interest. Refuted by that it assists reason in what it is independent and cooperates with it in what it is not independent [of] possibility in what is conjectured, and is the way in what is not perceived, while entrusting to differing intellects is a place for the disturbance of order. The third: That it is based on obligation by what the slave does not benefit from due to his harm, nor the worshipped due to His transcendence, along with what is in it of preoccupying the heart from complete attention. Refuted by that its benefit is greatly predominant. The fourth: That in the laws there is what indicates that they are not from God, such as the acts of prayer, pilgrimage, ablution, and washing, and other matters outside the law of reason (2). Refuted by that they are...
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage presents four objections from deniers of prophethood and their refutations. The first objection is that prophethood requires the prophet to know that the sender is God, which is impossible without prior knowledge. The refutation is that God can either establish proofs or create necessary knowledge to achieve this. The second objection is that prophethood is futile because reason alone can determine good and evil, so revelation is unnecessary. The refutation is that revelation assists reason in areas where reason is insufficient and removes doubt in conjectural matters, and it provides guidance in matters beyond sense perception. Furthermore, entrusting everything to differing intellects would lead to disorder. The third objection is that prophets impose obligations that bring no benefit to the slave or to God, and distract from devotion. The refutation is that the benefit of revelation is very great and predominant. The fourth objection, which is incomplete in the text, states that many religious practices seem irrational and indicate that they are not from God. The refutation, though cut off, likely explains that these are pure acts of worship known through revelation and beyond rational analysis.

Key Terms
تكليف
نفع
ضرر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophetic obligations are useless and harmful.
Correction: They have overwhelming benefit, especially spiritual and eternal.

Checkpoint
What is the refutation to the third objection that prophethood imposes useless obligations?
A) That obligations are not real
B) That the benefit is greatly predominant
C) That God is not affected
D) That the slave will be compensated

Key Takeaway
The benefit of divine obligations is overwhelmingly predominant, outweighing any apparent harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your religious duties are a burden or that they distract you from a more 'spiritual' connection with God? In light of this lesson, what specific benefit might you be overlooking in that obligation?
?
Think of a difficulty you have faced while fulfilling a command of Allah. How does the assertion that 'its benefit is greatly predominant' challenge your perception of that trial? Will you commit to seeking that benefit with sincerity?
?
If you truly believed that every obligation carries an overwhelmingly predominant benefit, what would change in the way you approach your prayers, fasting, or other duties starting today?

Objection 4: Irrational Religious Practices
Fourth objection and incomplete refutation: irrational laws.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection that religious laws contain irrational elements, and note that the refutation is incomplete in this passage.

Core Idea
Some religious practices are beyond rational justification, accepted through revelation.

The fourth objection points to acts like prayer, pilgrimage, and ablution as appearing irrational, suggesting they are not from God. Unfortunately, the text cuts off before completing the refutation. However, we can infer that these acts are accepted as pure acts of worship known only through revelation.

Guided Path
1

Read the Arabic for objection 4. It lists specific acts that seem irrational.
2
Note the incomplete refutation
The refutation is cut off after 'رد بأنها'. Understand that the argument is incomplete in this source.
The refutation likely continues on the next page.
3
Consider possible refutations
Think about how theologians justify apparently irrational acts: as purely devotional (ta'abbudi) or as having hidden wisdom.
This is a common objection against divine law.

Types of Deniers of Prophethood
Al-Taftazani categorizes deniers of prophethood into four groups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different categories of people who deny prophethood and why some are more significant than others.

Core Idea
Deniers of prophethood fall into distinct groups with varying levels of rational engagement.

Al-Taftazani begins his refutation by classifying the various types of deniers. This categorization helps distinguish between those whose objections deserve serious consideration and those who are simply obstinate or misguided. Understanding this classification prepares us for the specific objections that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the four groups
Read the Arabic passage and note the four categories of deniers: those who deem prophethood impossible, those who deem it unnecessary (Brahmins), those whose beliefs entail denial (philosophers), and those whose actions and words show denial (debauched individuals).
The Brahmins are highlighted as a distinct group from India.
2
Consider why Taftazani dismisses the first group
Notice that he dismisses the first group (those who say it's impossible) with 'لا اعتداد بهم' meaning they are not counted. Reflect on why they are not worth refuting.
This sets the stage for focusing on the more rational objections.
3
Understand the significance of the final sentence
The last sentence emphasizes that these deniers are not a coherent sect but scattered individuals. This frames the subsequent objections as intellectual doubts rather than systematic doctrines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنكرون للنبوة منهم من قال باستحالتها، ولا اعتداد بهم، ومنهم من قال بعدم الاحتياج إليها كالبراهمة (جمع من الهند أصحاب برهام) ومنهم من لزم ذلك من عقائدهم كالفلاسفة النافين لاختيار الباري وعلمه بالجزئيات، وظهور الملك على البشر، ونزوله من السموات ومنهم من لاح ذلك من على أفعاله وأقواله كالمصرين على الخلاعة وعدم المبالاة، وفي التكاليف ، ودلالة المعجزات، وهؤلاء آحاد أوباش من الطوائف، لا طائفة معينة يكون لها ملة ونحلة. وبالجملة للمنكرين شبه : الأولى: أن البعثة تتوقف على علم المبعوث بأن الباعث هو الله تعالى، ولا سبيل إلى ذلك، والجواب المنع لجواز أن ينصب دليلاً له أو يخلق علماً ضرورياً فيه. الثانية : وهي للبراهمة أن ما جاء به النبي إما أن يكون موافقاً للعقل حسناً عنده، فيقبل ويفعل ، وإن لم يكن نبياً أو مخالفاً له قبيحاً عند فيرد ويترك. وإن جاء به النبي وأياً ما كان لا حاجة إليه، فإن قيل : لعله لا يكون حسناً عند العقل، ولا قبيحاً، قلنا: فيفعل عند الحاجة لأن مجرد الاحتمال لا يعارض تنجز الاحتياج، ويترك عند عدمها للاحتياط والجواب : أن ما يوافق العقل قد يستقل بمعرفته فيعاضده النبي ويؤكده . بمنزلة الأدلة العقلية على مدلول واحد وقد لا يستقل فيدله عليه ويرشده، وما يخالف العقل قد لا يكون مع الجزم فيدفعه النبي أو يرفع عنه الاحتمال، وما لا يدرك حسنه ولا قبحه قد يكون حسناً يجب فعله أو قبيحاً يجب تركه، هذا مع أن العقول متفاوتة، فالتفويض إليها مظنة التنازع والتقاتل ومفض إلى اختلال النظام، وأن فوائد البعثة لا تنحصر في بيان حسن الأشياء وقبحها على ما تقدم. أحد بقوله : أنا وقيل العقل جوهر روحاني خلقه الله تعالى متعلقا بالبدن، وقيل العقل نور في القلب يعرف الحق والباطل، وقيل العقل جوهر مجرد عن المادة يتعلق بالبدن تعلق التدبير والتصرف وقيل العقل قوة النفس الناطقة، والله أعلم .
The deniers of prophethood: some say it is impossible, and they are not to be counted; some say there is no need for it, like the Brahmins (a group from India, followers of Brahma); some are necessitated by their beliefs, like the philosophers who deny the choice of the Creator and His knowledge of particulars, the appearance of an angel to humans, and his descent from the heavens; and some show that by their actions and words, like those insistent on debauchery and lack of concern regarding obligations and the evidence of miracles. These are individuals and rabble from the sects, not a specific group having a religion and creed. In sum, the deniers have doubts: First: the prophetic mission depends on the sent one knowing that the sender is God Almighty, and there is no way to that. The answer is the denial, because it is permissible that He set up a proof for him or create necessary knowledge in him. Second: and it is from the Brahmins, that what the prophet brings is either in agreement with reason, good according to it, so it is accepted and done even if he is not a prophet, or contradictory to it, evil according to it, so it is rejected and abandoned. And if the prophet brings it, whichever it is, there is no need for him. If it is said: perhaps it is neither good nor evil according to reason, we say: then it is done when needed, because mere probability does not oppose the realization of need, and left when not needed for caution. The answer: that what agrees with reason may be independently known, so the prophet aids and confirms it, like rational proofs for a single conclusion; or it may not be independent, so he indicates it and guides to it. What opposes reason may not be certain, so the prophet removes it or lifts the probability. What is not perceived as good or evil may be good and obligatory to do or evil and obligatory to avoid. This, while intellects are varied, so delegating to them is a ground for conflict and fighting and leads to disturbance of order. And the benefits of the prophetic mission are not limited to clarifying the goodness and evil of things as mentioned. One said: I, and it is said intellect is a spiritual substance created by God, attached to the body; and it is said intellect is a light in the heart by which truth and falsehood are known; and it is said intellect is a substance abstracted from matter, attached to the body in the manner of management and disposal; and it is said intellect is a power of the rational soul. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage categorizes deniers of prophethood and provides a detailed refutation of the first two objections. The author first divides deniers into groups: those who claim prophethood is impossible (dismissed as negligible), those who claim it is unnecessary (like the Brahmins), those whose beliefs entail denial (philosophers denying God's choice and knowledge of particulars, and the possibility of angelic appearance), and those whose actions show denial (debauched individuals). These are scattered individuals, not a coherent sect. Then he presents the first objection again: the prophet must know the sender is God, but there is no way to that. The answer: God can set up a proof or create necessary knowledge in him. The second objection, from the Brahmins, argues that if a prophet brings what reason approves, it is accepted anyway; if he brings what reason disapproves, it is rejected; and if neutral, it is done when needed. Therefore, no need for a prophet. The answer expands: reason may be independent in some matters, so the prophet confirms and aids it; in others, reason is not independent, so the prophet guides it; for matters reason cannot judge, the prophet informs. Moreover, intellects vary, so delegating to them leads to conflict and disorder. Thus, the benefits of prophethood extend far beyond merely clarifying good and evil; it provides guidance, confirmation, and social order.

Key Terms
البراهمة
الملة
النحلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All deniers of prophethood are a unified group with a single argument.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows they are diverse and many are not worth refuting; only the Brahmins and similar rational objectors are engaged.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which group of deniers does Al-Taftazani dismiss as not worth counting?
A) The Brahmins
B) Those who say prophethood is impossible
C) The philosophers
D) The debauched individuals

Key Takeaway
Deniers of prophethood are not a homogenous group; they range from those who deem it impossible to those whose lifestyles lead them to reject it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the four categories of deniers do you find closest to your own heart? Do you ever live as if prophethood is unnecessary—maybe by neglecting the Sunnah when it is inconvenient? Take one habit this week where you consciously submit to a prophetic teaching that conflicts with your personal preference.
?
The philosophers denied prophethood because of intellectual pride. In what area of your life do you trust your own reasoning over the guidance of the Prophet? When you encounter a hadith that challenges your view, do you pause or dismiss it? Write down one such issue and commit to seeking the prophetic answer first.
?
The Brahmins claimed their intellect was sufficient, feeling no need for a prophet. How often do you truly feel you need the Prophet? Do you seek his guidance for every big decision—your career, marriage, finances—or only in ritual matters? Name one decision you are facing that you will deliberately align with the Prophet's guidance, even if your intellect suggests otherwise.

Refutation of the First Objection: Knowledge of the Sender
Al-Taftazani states the first objection and gives a concise answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to prophethood and how Al-Taftazani refutes it by showing that God can provide the necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
The prophet can know God is the sender through divine proofs or direct knowledge.

The first objection concerns the epistemological basis of prophethood: how can a prophet be certain that the one sending him is God? Al-Taftazani presents this objection and answers it succinctly, relying on God's power to create certainty either through external proofs or immediate knowledge. This refutation demonstrates that the objection is not unanswerable.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the premise: the prophetic mission depends on the prophet knowing that the sender is God. The objection claims there is no way to that knowledge.
2
Understand Al-Taftazani's answer
He responds with 'المنع' (denial) and provides two possibilities: God can set up a proof (like a miracle) or create necessary knowledge directly in the prophet.
Notice the concise style of refutation.
3
Reflect on the significance
Why is this answer sufficient? It shows that God is not limited in means of communication. The objection assumes human incapacity, but divine power overcomes it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The author first divides deniers into groups: those who claim prophethood is impossible (dismissed as negligible), those who claim it is unnecessary (like the Brahmins), those whose beliefs entail denial (philosophers denying God's choice and knowledge of particulars, and the possibility of angelic appearance), and those whose actions show denial (debauched individuals). These are scattered individuals, not a coherent sect. Then he presents the first objection again: the prophet must know the sender is God, but there is no way to that. The answer: God can set up a proof or create necessary knowledge in him. The second objection, from the Brahmins, argues that if a prophet brings what reason approves, it is accepted anyway; if he brings what reason disapproves, it is rejected; and if neutral, it is done when needed. Therefore, no need for a prophet. The answer expands: reason may be independent in some matters, so the prophet confirms and aids it; in others, reason is not independent, so the prophet guides it; for matters reason cannot judge, the prophet informs. Moreover, intellects vary, so delegating to them leads to conflict and disorder. Thus, the benefits of prophethood extend far beyond merely clarifying good and evil; it provides guidance, confirmation, and social order.

Key Terms
البعثة
المبعوث
الباعث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A prophet cannot know for sure that God is sending him.
Correction: Al-Taftazani states that God can provide proof or direct knowledge, making certain knowledge possible.

Checkpoint

A) He admits it's impossible but says it's still necessary to believe
B) He denies the impossibility and suggests God can provide a proof or necessary knowledge
C) He argues that the prophet already knows God innately
D) He says the prophet relies on community consensus

Key Takeaway
The first objection to prophethood is refuted by affirming God's ability to give the prophet certainty through either a miracle or direct knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face doubts about the truth of Islam, do you expect a miracle or do you seek intellectual proof? How does this lesson's claim that God can create certainty directly challenge your approach to faith?
?
If God can give prophets direct, undeniable knowledge of His message, why do you think He does not do the same for everyone? What does this teach you about the role of your own effort in seeking certainty?
?
Imagine you received a direct, undeniable sign from God. How would your life change? Are you living in a way that shows you already believe you have received such a sign through the Qur'an and the Prophet? If not, what is holding you back?

Refutation of the Second Objection: Sufficiency of Reason
Al-Taftazani refutes the Brahmin objection that reason suffices for guidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Brahmins' argument that reason makes prophethood unnecessary, and Al-Taftazani's multifaceted refutation showing reason's limitations and the benefits of prophecy.

Core Idea
Reason alone is insufficient; prophethood provides confirmation, guidance, and social order.

The Brahmins argue that whatever a prophet brings is either already known by reason (so no need) or opposed to reason (so rejected), or neutral (so decided by need). Al-Taftazani counters by showing that reason is not always independent, that prophets confirm and clarify, and that relying solely on reason leads to conflict. He also notes that some moral truths are not known by reason alone. This lesson unpacks that response and its implications for the necessity of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the Brahmin objection
Identify the tripartite division: matters agreeable to reason, contrary to reason, and neutral. The Brahmins claim none of these require a prophet.
Notice the 'if it is said' scenario for neutral matters.
2
Understand Al-Taftazani's answer
He breaks down answer into three parts: matters reason may or may not independently know; matters contrary to reason may be uncertain; neutral matters may be morally obligatory or forbidden. Then he adds the argument from diversity of intellects and social disorder.
This is a comprehensive response.
3
Reflect on the final point about diversity of intellects
Why does this strengthen the refutation? Because human reason varies, and delegating moral guidance to reason alone leads to conflict and breakdown of order.
This shows prophethood as a unifying force.
4
Consider the footnote on definitions of 'aql
The passage ends with various definitions of intellect. This shows that 'aql is not a simple concept, and its role in ethics is complex.
These definitions are from different schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنكرون للنبوة منهم من قال باستحالتها، ولا اعتداد بهم، ومنهم من قال بعدم الاحتياج إليها كالبراهمة (جمع من الهند أصحاب برهام) ومنهم من لزم ذلك من عقائدهم كالفلاسفة النافين لاختيار الباري وعلمه بالجزئيات، وظهور الملك على البشر، ونزوله من السموات ومنهم من لاح ذلك من على أفعاله وأقواله كالمصرين على الخلاعة وعدم المبالاة، وفي التكاليف ، ودلالة المعجزات، وهؤلاء آحاد أوباش من الطوائف، لا طائفة معينة يكون لها ملة ونحلة. وبالجملة للمنكرين شبه : الأولى: أن البعثة تتوقف على علم المبعوث بأن الباعث هو الله تعالى، ولا سبيل إلى ذلك، والجواب المنع لجواز أن ينصب دليلاً له أو يخلق علماً ضرورياً فيه. الثانية : وهي للبراهمة أن ما جاء به النبي إما أن يكون موافقاً للعقل حسناً عنده، فيقبل ويفعل ، وإن لم يكن نبياً أو مخالفاً له قبيحاً عند فيرد ويترك. وإن جاء به النبي وأياً ما كان لا حاجة إليه، فإن قيل : لعله لا يكون حسناً عند العقل، ولا قبيحاً، قلنا: فيفعل عند الحاجة لأن مجرد الاحتمال لا يعارض تنجز الاحتياج، ويترك عند عدمها للاحتياط والجواب : أن ما يوافق العقل قد يستقل بمعرفته فيعاضده النبي ويؤكده . بمنزلة الأدلة العقلية على مدلول واحد وقد لا يستقل فيدله عليه ويرشده، وما يخالف العقل قد لا يكون مع الجزم فيدفعه النبي أو يرفع عنه الاحتمال، وما لا يدرك حسنه ولا قبحه قد يكون حسناً يجب فعله أو قبيحاً يجب تركه، هذا مع أن العقول متفاوتة، فالتفويض إليها مظنة التنازع والتقاتل ومفض إلى اختلال النظام، وأن فوائد البعثة لا تنحصر في بيان حسن الأشياء وقبحها على ما تقدم. أحد بقوله : أنا وقيل العقل جوهر روحاني خلقه الله تعالى متعلقا بالبدن، وقيل العقل نور في القلب يعرف الحق والباطل، وقيل العقل جوهر مجرد عن المادة يتعلق بالبدن تعلق التدبير والتصرف وقيل العقل قوة النفس الناطقة، والله أعلم .
The deniers of prophethood: some say it is impossible, and they are not to be counted; some say there is no need for it, like the Brahmins (a group from India, followers of Brahma); some are necessitated by their beliefs, like the philosophers who deny the choice of the Creator and His knowledge of particulars, the appearance of an angel to humans, and his descent from the heavens; and some show that by their actions and words, like those insistent on debauchery and lack of concern regarding obligations and the evidence of miracles. These are individuals and rabble from the sects, not a specific group having a religion and creed. In sum, the deniers have doubts: First: the prophetic mission depends on the sent one knowing that the sender is God Almighty, and there is no way to that. The answer is the denial, because it is permissible that He set up a proof for him or create necessary knowledge in him. Second: and it is from the Brahmins, that what the prophet brings is either in agreement with reason, good according to it, so it is accepted and done even if he is not a prophet, or contradictory to it, evil according to it, so it is rejected and abandoned. And if the prophet brings it, whichever it is, there is no need for him. If it is said: perhaps it is neither good nor evil according to reason, we say: then it is done when needed, because mere probability does not oppose the realization of need, and left when not needed for caution. The answer: that what agrees with reason may be independently known, so the prophet aids and confirms it, like rational proofs for a single conclusion; or it may not be independent, so he indicates it and guides to it. What opposes reason may not be certain, so the prophet removes it or lifts the probability. What is not perceived as good or evil may be good and obligatory to do or evil and obligatory to avoid. This, while intellects are varied, so delegating to them is a ground for conflict and fighting and leads to disturbance of order. And the benefits of the prophetic mission are not limited to clarifying the goodness and evil of things as mentioned. One said: I, and it is said intellect is a spiritual substance created by God, attached to the body; and it is said intellect is a light in the heart by which truth and falsehood are known; and it is said intellect is a substance abstracted from matter, attached to the body in the manner of management and disposal; and it is said intellect is a power of the rational soul. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage categorizes deniers of prophethood and provides a detailed refutation of the first two objections. The author first divides deniers into groups: those who claim prophethood is impossible (dismissed as negligible), those who claim it is unnecessary (like the Brahmins), those whose beliefs entail denial (philosophers denying God's choice and knowledge of particulars, and the possibility of angelic appearance), and those whose actions show denial (debauched individuals). These are scattered individuals, not a coherent sect. Then he presents the first objection again: the prophet must know the sender is God, but there is no way to that. The answer: God can set up a proof or create necessary knowledge in him. The second objection, from the Brahmins, argues that if a prophet brings what reason approves, it is accepted anyway; if he brings what reason disapproves, it is rejected; and if neutral, it is done when needed. Therefore, no need for a prophet. The answer expands: reason may be independent in some matters, so the prophet confirms and aids it; in others, reason is not independent, so the prophet guides it; for matters reason cannot judge, the prophet informs. Moreover, intellects vary, so delegating to them leads to conflict and disorder. Thus, the benefits of prophethood extend far beyond merely clarifying good and evil; it provides guidance, confirmation, and social order.

The First Objection: Knowing the Sender of Prophethood
Objection and answer: necessity of knowing the sender.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first major objection against the possibility of prophethood and how classical scholars answered it by appealing to divine provision of evidence.

Core Idea
Prophethood requires certain knowledge of God; but God can provide that knowledge directly.

This short passage encapsulates one of the earliest objections to the possibility of prophethood: how can a person know for certain that the one sending them is God? The answer is not to prove that knowledge is impossible, but to show that God can supply it through miracles or direct creation. Let's examine the argument together.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: 'The first: that ... depends on ... and there is no way.'
2
Identify the two components
Identify the condition (knowledge of the sender) and the impossibility claim. Then read the answer: 'prevention, because it is possible...'
The scholar refutes by rejecting the premise that there is no way.
3
Reflect on the method of refutation
Ask yourself why the answer works. The objector claims knowledge is impossible, but the responder shows two possibilities: a proof or direct knowledge.
This is a typical kalam style: rejecting the premise (al-man').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأولى: أن البعثة تتوقف على علم المبعوث بأن الباعث هو الله تعالى، ولا سبيل إلى ذلك، والجواب المنع لجواز أن ينصب دليلاً له أو يخلق علماً ضرورياً فيه.
The first: that prophethood depends on the knowledge of the one sent that the sender is God Almighty, and there is no way to that. The answer: prevention, because it is possible that He appoints a proof for him or creates necessary knowledge in him.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection (shubha) against prophethood and its answer. The objection states that prophethood (baʿtha) requires that the recipient (al-mabʿūth) know with certainty that the sender is God, but there is no way to acquire such knowledge. The answer quickly refutes this by 'prevention' (al-manʿ), meaning the premise is not accepted. It is possible for God to either appoint a proof (dalīl) for the recipient—such as a miracle—or create necessary knowledge (ʿilm ḍarūrī) directly in his mind, thereby removing the difficulty.

Key Terms
بعثة
مبعوث
باعث
دليل
علم ضروري
منع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the only way to know the sender is through direct perception or reasoning, making prophethood impossible without such evidence.
Correction: The objection fails because God can create the necessary knowledge directly or provide a proof (miracle), so the required knowledge is attainable.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection against prophethood mentioned here?
A) That the prophet might be an impostor.
B) That the recipient cannot know the sender is God.
C) That reason is sufficient without prophets.
D) That miracles are impossible.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because God can provide the necessary knowledge through a miracle or direct creation, thus the requirement of prophethood is not impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson contrasts external proof (like miracles) and inner direct knowledge. Which do you lean on more in your own faith, and why? Does your reliance show more trust in God or in your own reasoning?
?
If God were to create necessary knowledge in you right now, would you be ready to accept it without reservation? What would need to change in your heart for you to submit to such direct divine intervention?

The Barahima's Objection: Reason is Sufficient
The Barahima's objection that reason is sufficient

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Barahima's argument that reason alone can judge all actions, making prophethood unnecessary.

Core Idea
The Barahima claim reason can judge all, so a prophet adds nothing.

The Barahima, an ancient Indian sect, presented a powerful objection against the necessity of prophethood. They argued that whatever a prophet brings is either already known by reason or opposed to it—in either case, the prophet is redundant. This lesson examines their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the logical structure: the dilemma and the response to indeterminate actions.
The Barahima use a dichotomy: either reason approves or disapproves.
2
Identify the core claim
Find the sentence that states the prophet is unnecessary regardless of what he brings.
The phrase 'وإن جاء به النبي وأياً ما كان لا حاجة إليه' is the key.
3
Understand the handling of indeterminate actions
The Barahima consider actions that are neither clearly good nor ugly in reason. Their solution: act when needed, omit when not, based on probability.
They use 'الاحتمال' to argue that mere possibility doesn't overturn need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية : وهي للبراهمة أن ما جاء به النبي إما أن يكون موافقاً للعقل حسناً عنده، فيقبل ويفعل، وإن لم يكن نبياً أو مخالفاً له قبيحاً عند فيرد ويترك. وإن جاء به النبي وأياً ما كان لا حاجة إليه، فإن قيل : لعله لا يكون حسناً عند العقل، ولا قبيحاً، قلنا: فيفعل عند الحاجة لأن مجرد الاحتمال لا يعارض تنجز الاحتياج، ويترك عند عدمها للاحتياط والجواب : أن ما يوافق العقل قد يستقل بمعرفته فيعاضده النبي ويؤكده . بمنزلة الأدلة العقلية على مدلول واحد وقد لا يستقل فيدله عليه ويرشده، وما يخالف العقل قد لا يكون مع الجزم فيدفعه النبي أو يرفع عنه الاحتمال، وما لا يدرك حسنه ولا قبحه قد يكون حسناً يجب فعله أو قبيحاً يجب تركه، هذا مع أن العقول متفاوتة، فالتفويض إليها مظنة التنازع والتقاتل ومفض إلى اختلال النظام، وأن فوائد البعثة لا تنحصر في بيان حسن الأشياء وقبحها على ما تقدم.
The second: and it is from the Barahima that what the prophet brings is either in accordance with reason, good in its view, so it is accepted and done even if he were not a prophet, or opposed to it, ugly in its view, so it is rejected and left. And whether the prophet brings it or not, there is no need for him. If it is said: perhaps it is neither good in reason's view nor ugly, we say: it is done when needed because mere probability does not oppose the actualization of need, and left when not needed as a precaution. The answer: that what is in accordance with reason may be independently known by it, so the prophet confirms and strengthens it, like rational proofs for a single conclusion; and it may not be independent, so he indicates and guides to it. And what opposes reason may not be with certainty, so the prophet removes it or removes the probability. And what is not perceived as good or ugly may be good and must be done or ugly and must be avoided. This is besides that intellects differ, so referring to them is a cause of dispute and fighting and leads to disorder, and the benefits of prophethood are not limited to explaining the goodness and ugliness of things as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection from the Barahima (a group from India) who claim that reason is sufficient and prophethood is unnecessary. They argue that whatever a prophet brings is either in accordance with reason—so it is accepted regardless of a prophet—or opposed to reason—so it is rejected regardless. If something is neither good nor ugly in reason, it can be acted upon when needed or left as a precaution. Thus, prophethood has no role. The author's answer is detailed: first, what agrees with reason may be independently known by reason, in which case the prophet confirms it (like multiple proofs for one point), or may not be independently known, so the prophet teaches and guides to it. What opposes reason may not be certain to be false, so the prophet removes doubt or indicates its falsehood. What is not perceived as good or ugly may actually be good (obligatory) or ugly (forbidden), and reason alone cannot know this. Additionally, intellects vary, leading to conflict, so referring to them is dangerous. The benefits of prophethood are not limited to moral teaching but include many others.

Key Terms
براهمة
عقل
حسن
قبح
بعثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Barahima's argument is easily dismissed as irrational.
Correction: The Barahima present a logical dilemma that must be answered; it is not obviously false, and a serious refutation is required.

Checkpoint
According to the Barahima, what is the role of the prophet if something is already good in reason?
A) He must be followed to confirm it.
B) It is accepted regardless of him.
C) He makes it obligatory.
D) He changes its moral status.

Key Takeaway
The Barahima argue that reason is fully independent, making prophethood irrelevant for moral guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself relying solely on your own reasoning to decide what is right and wrong, ignoring what Allah has revealed through His prophets? In what specific area of your life does this happen most often? What will you change today to submit to revelation over your own intellect?
?
If the Barahima were right and you had no need for prophets, would your practice of Islam – like prayer, fasting, or giving charity – be motivated by any reason other than obedience to Allah? Would you still do these acts if your reason told you they were unnecessary?
?
Think of a command from the Quran or Sunnah that your intellect struggles to accept as good. According to the Barahima, you would reject it. But as a believer, you must submit. Where is your faith weakest in following a revealed command that contradicts your reasoning? Will you commit to obeying it starting now, even if you don't fully understand the wisdom?

Refuting the Barahima: The Role of Prophethood
The author's refutation of the Barahima's objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multifaceted refutation of the Barahima, highlighting the limits of reason and the essential functions of prophethood.

Core Idea
Reason is limited; prophecy confirms, clarifies, and reveals what reason cannot.

The author provides a detailed answer to the Barahima. He shows that reason is not fully independent: it may need confirmation, it may be uncertain, and it cannot perceive the moral status of many actions. Moreover, intellects differ, leading to conflict, and prophecy's benefits extend beyond moral teaching.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic from 'والجواب' to the end, then the translation. Note the three categories of actions and the additional arguments.
The refutation is structured: first, what agrees with reason; second, what opposes it; third, what is beyond reason's scope.
2
Analyze the three cases
For each case, note how the prophet's role differs: confirmation, removal of doubt, or teaching the unknown.
The third case is crucial: actions whose goodness/ugliness is not rationally perceived.
3
Consider the supplementary arguments
The author adds that intellects vary (leading to conflict) and that prophethood has broader benefits.
This shows the weakness of relying solely on reason.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reason can independently know all moral truths.
Correction: Many actions are not rationally perceptible as good or ugly; prophecy is needed to reveal their moral status.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what is the role of the prophet regarding actions whose goodness/ugliness is not perceived by reason?
A) The prophet confirms them if they are already known.
B) The prophet removes doubt about them.
C) The prophet reveals that they may be obligatory or forbidden.
D) The prophet leaves them to reason's judgment.

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is necessary because reason is limited, intellects differ, and many moral truths are beyond rational perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The passage warns that differing intellects lead to conflict and disorder. Where in your relationships or community do you see this happening? How can you actively turn to prophetic guidance as a unifying authority instead of insisting on your own opinion?

The Objection that Taklif is Futile and Its Refutation
Objection that taklif is futile and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection to prophethood (that divine command is futile) and its refutation based on the outweighing benefits of taklif.

Core Idea
Taklif's temporary hardships are outweighed by eternal benefits.

The third objection claims that religious obligation (taklif) is pointless—it harms the servant and cannot benefit God. Moreover, it distracts from the ultimate goal of knowing Him. The refutation shows that the harms are minimal compared to the immense benefits, and that taklif actually directs one toward that goal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage carefully, which presents the third objection.
Notice the three specific arguments given.
2
Identify the three points of the objection
Find the three reasons: no benefit for the servant (only harm), no benefit for God, and distraction from the ultimate goal.
3
Read the refutation
Read the answer provided in the passage, which responds to the objection.
4
Analyze the refutation's logic
See how the refutation counters each point: harms are few, benefits are many and evident, and taklif actually turns toward the goal, not away.
It also distinguishes between those who see only outward aspects and those who see hidden secrets.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this refutation is crucial for defending the wisdom of prophethood and divine command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة : أن العمدة في باب البعثة هي التكليف، وهو عبث لا يليق بالحكيم، إذ لا يشتمل على فائدة للعبد لكونه في حقه مضرة ناجزة ومشقة ظاهرة، ولا للمعبود لتعاليه الاستفادة والانتفاع ، وأيضاً فيه شغل للقلب عما هو غاية الأعمال ونهاية الكمال، عن أعني الاستغراق في معرفته والغناء في عظمته والجواب : أن مضاره الناجزة قليلة جداً بالنسبة إلى منافعها الدنيوية والأخروية الظاهرة لدى الواقفين على ظواهر الشريعة النبوية فضلاً عن الكاشفين عن أسرارها الخفية، وإذا تأملتم فالتكليف صرف إلى ما ذكرتم لا شغل عنه على ما توهمتم .
The third: that the foundation in the matter of prophethood is taklif (divine command), and it is futile, unbefitting the Wise, for it contains no benefit for the servant because it is immediate harm and evident hardship, nor for the Worshipped because He is exalted above receiving benefit and profit. Also in it is an occupation of the heart from what is the goal of deeds and the perfection of perfection, I mean absorption in His knowledge and immersion in His greatness. The answer: that its immediate harms are very few compared to its worldly and otherworldly benefits evident to those who know the outward aspects of the prophetic law, let alone those who uncover its hidden secrets. And if you reflect, taklif turns toward what you mentioned, not an occupation from it as you imagined.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection: that taklif (religious obligation) is the main point of prophethood, but it is futile (ʿabath) and unworthy of a wise God. The objection argues that taklif has no benefit for the servant—only immediate harm and hardship—and no benefit for God, who is far above needing anything. It also distracts the heart from the ultimate goal of absorption in God's knowledge and greatness. The answer refutes this by pointing out that the immediate harms are negligible compared to the vast worldly and otherworldly benefits, which are evident even to those who only understand the outward aspects of the revealed law, let alone those who penetrate its hidden secrets. Moreover, taklif actually directs one toward that goal, not away from it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taklif is purely burdensome with no benefit, making it futile and unworthy of God.
Correction: Taklif's immediate harms are negligible compared to its immense worldly and otherworldly benefits. It is not futile but a means to attain the ultimate goal of knowing God.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the third objection?
A) Miracles are impossible.
B) Prophethood is unnecessary because reason is sufficient.
C) Taklif is useless and harmful, with no benefit.
D) The prophet might be an impostor.

Key Takeaway
Taklif is not futile; its harms are negligible compared to its immense benefits, and it directs the heart toward God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a religious duty that feels burdensome, do you genuinely believe that its harm is negligible compared to its eternal benefit? If not, what is holding you back from that conviction, and what will you do to change your perspective starting today?
?
The objection claims that taklif occupies the heart away from God. Honestly examine your own experience: Do your religious rituals often feel like a distraction from deeper connection, or do they lead you to absorption in Him? What practical step can you take to turn them from mere duty into a path to intimacy?
?
If you truly internalized that every command from God comes with immense hidden benefits, would you still complain about its difficulty? Write down one obligation you resent, and then ask God to show you its secret benefit—and commit to fulfilling it with gratitude this week.

Fourth Objection: Arbitrariness of Rituals
Objection and answer about ritual arbitrariness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that religious rituals are arbitrary and the scholarly response that they are devotional tests with hidden wisdom.

Core Idea
Religious rituals are not arbitrary; they are tests that strengthen obedience and contain hidden wisdom.

The objection that religious rituals are irrational and meaningless has been raised by those who follow their desires. This lesson presents the objection and its refutation, showing that such acts are devotional trials designed to cultivate obedience and may have benefits known only to God and the deeply learned.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and translation focusing on the objection from the people of libertinism. Notice how they argue that a wise Maker would not prescribe such acts.
The objection is based on apparent irrationality of rituals like Hajj and prayer.
2
Understand the answer
Identify the key elements of the answer: devotional nature (taʿabbudiyyāt), purpose of test (ibtilāʾ), subjugation of souls, strengthening compliance (malaka), and hidden wisdoms.
The answer also references 'those firmly rooted in knowledge' who may perceive hidden secrets.
3
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider why the author classifies these acts as taʿabbudiyyāt and how this responds to the charge of arbitrariness. Think about the role of faith in accepting such commands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة : وهي لأهل الخلاعة المنهمكين في اتباع الهوى وترك الطاعة أنا نجد الشرائع مشتملة على أفعال وهيئات لا يشك في أن الصانع الحكيم لا يعتبرها ولا يأمر بها كما نشاهد في الحج ، والصلاة، وكغسل بعض الأعضاء لتلوث بعض آخر، إلى الأمور الخارجة عن قانون العقل والجواب : أنها أمور تعبدية اعتبرها الشارع ابتلاء للمكلفين، وتطويعاً لنفوسهم وتأكيداً لملكة امتثالهم الأوامر والنواهي . ولعل فيها حكماً ومصالح لا يعلمها إلا الله والراسخون في العلم " وقد أشار إليها بعض الخائضين في بحار أسرار الشريعة.
The fourth: and it is from the people of libertinism who are immersed in following desire and abandoning obedience: we find the revealed laws containing acts and postures about which there is no doubt that the Wise Maker does not regard them nor command them, as we witness in Hajj, prayer, washing some limbs due to the soiling of others, and matters outside the law of reason. The answer: that they are devotional matters (taʿabbudiyyāt) considered by the Lawgiver as a trial for the obligated, a subjugation of their souls, and a strengthening of the firm trait (malaka) of their compliance with commands and prohibitions. And perhaps in them are wisdoms and interests known only to God and those firmly rooted in knowledge. And some of those who dive into the seas of the secrets of the Law have alluded to them.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection from the 'people of libertinism' (ahl al-khalāʿa) who are given to following their desires and avoiding obedience. They argue that the revealed laws contain acts and postures that a wise Maker would not prescribe, such as the details of Hajj, prayer, washing limbs, etc.—things that seem contrary to rational law. The answer is that these are devotional matters (taʿabbudiyyāt) that the Lawgiver has prescribed as a test (ibtilāʾ) for the believers, to subdue their souls (taṭwīʿan) and to strengthen their trait (malaka) of compliance. Additionally, there may be hidden wisdoms and benefits known only to God and those deeply learned in the faith; some scholars have hinted at such secrets.

الراسخون في العلم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think religious rituals are arbitrary and pointless.
Correction: They serve as tests and contain hidden wisdom, and are not arbitrary.

Checkpoint

A) By denying the existence of such rituals.
B) By claiming they are rational in themselves.
C) By stating they are devotional tests that strengthen obedience.
D) By admitting they are arbitrary but necessary.

Key Takeaway
Religious rituals are not meaningless; they are devotional acts prescribed by God as tests and to cultivate obedience, and they may contain hidden wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a religious command or ritual you have struggled to understand or found meaningless. Have you ever questioned its value or complained inwardly? After this lesson, will you now see it as a divine test for your obedience? What concrete step will you take to change your attitude towards that command?
?
The lesson says rituals are meant to 'subjugate your soul' and 'strengthen compliance.' Which aspect of your soul—your desires, ego, or intellect—most resists submitting to Allah's commands without demanding rational justification? How will you confront that resistance this week?
?
Imagine you stand before Allah, and He asks you about a ritual you neglected because it seemed pointless. Can you offer an excuse? After learning that these acts may contain wisdom beyond your understanding, what will you do differently starting today to ensure you do not fall into the trap of the 'people of libertinism'?
